            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been revived as important alternative energy storage devices for large-scale energy storage, which requires SIBs to have a long cycling life and high power d.  However, the scarcity of suitable anode materials hinders their application.  Herein, we report a bismuth intercalated graphite (Bi@Graphite) anode material, which is substantially different from the previously reported metal@Graphene.  In Bi@Graphite, the Bi nanoparticles between graphite interlayers enhance the capacity, while the graphite sheath provides a robust fast electronic connection for long cycling stability.  The Bi@Graphite possesses a safe av. storage potential of approx. 0.5 V vs.  Na/Na+, delivers a capacity of ∼160 mA h g-1 at 1C (160 mA g-1), exhibits outstanding cycling stability (ca. 90% capacity retention for 10 000 cycles at 20C), and can maintain 70% capacity at 300C (∼110 mA h g-1 at 48 A g-1), which is equiv. to full charge/discharge in 12 s.  Bi@Graphite demonstrates the highest rate-capability ever reported among all anodes for SIBs.  Detailed characterization results indicate that the unique Bi nanoparticle-in-graphite structure and the fast kinetics of ether co-intercalation into graphite are responsible for these significant improvements, which could translate into SIBs with excellent power densities.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoSO-lUIYzLkbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhTlVng7VMRGw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXmtVCisrs%253D&amp;md5=fad06b243c2fb803c4692029376e7f13</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA03444C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Harris, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qu, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dual Carbon-Protected Metal Sulfides and Their Application to Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal sulfides are considered as promising anode materials for sodium ion batteries owing to their good redox reversibility and relatively high theor. capacity.  However, their cycle life and rate capability are still unsatisfactory because of poor cond. and a large vol. change during the discharge/charge processes.  A facile method for prepg. dual carbon-protected metal sulfides is reported.  Metal diethyldithiocarbamate complexes are used as precursors.  The synthesis only involves a co-pptn. of metal diethyldithiocarbamate complexes with graphene oxide and a subsequent thermal pyrolysis.  As an example, N-doped carbon-coated iron sulfides wrapped in the graphene sheets (Fe1-xS@NC@G) are prepd. and used as the anode material for a sodium ion battery.  The as-synthesized Fe1-xS@NC@G electrode exhibits a high reversible capacity (440 mA h g-1 at 0.05 A g-1), outstanding cycling stability (95.8% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 0.2 A g-1), and good rate capability (243 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1).  Coupled with a Na3V2(PO4)2@C cathode, the full battery exhibits a high capacity retention ratio of 96.5% after 100 cycles and an av. output voltage of ca. 2.2 V.  More importantly, the proposed synthesis route is universal and can be extended to fabricate diverse transition metal sulfide-based composites with a dual carbon-protected nanostructure for advanced alkali ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoJW7tiJ6oHqLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljn-fK0TdPE9Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtFemtL7E&amp;md5=e83ce26b9e9076c6e43793ab3f8c775b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.  The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).  Excellent cycling stability is also obsd. at an even higher charge-discharge rate.  A high reversible capacity of 149 mA-h/g also can be obtained at a current rate of 500 mA/g (2C).  The good Na-ion insertion property is attributed to the short diffusion distance in the hollow carbon nanowires and the large interlayer distance (0.37 nm) between the graphitic sheets, which agrees with the interlayered distance predicted by theor. calcns. to enable Na-ion insertion in carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9utH1EdajJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljn-fK0TdPE9Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XotlShu78%253D&amp;md5=c54fcb809a340c023d9185f1d9f5e848</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.10.034"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Henderson, W.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon nanoparticles (HCNP) were synthesized by the pyrolysis of a polyaniline precursor.  The measured Na+ cation diffusion coeff. (10-13-10-15 cm2 s-1) in the HCNP obtained at 1150 °C is two orders of magnitude lower than that of Li+ in graphite (10-10-10-13 cm2 s-1), indicating that reducing the carbon particle size is very important for improving electrochem. performance.  These measurements also enable a clear visualization of the stepwise reaction phases and rate changes which occur throughout the insertion/extn. processes in HCNP, The electrochem. measurements also show that the nano-sized HCNP obtained at 1150 °C exhibited higher practical capacity at voltages lower than 1.2 V (vs. Na/Na+), as well as a prolonged cycling stability, which is attributed to an optimum spacing of 0.366 nm between the graphitic layers and the nano particular size resulting in a low-barrier Na+ cation insertion.  These results suggest that HCNP is a very promising high-capacity/stability anode for low cost sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0215ZE-6YJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljn-fK0TdPE9Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVaqtrrE&amp;md5=93cb764059ea5aaa3841222611c03416</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1039/c4cp00826j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tokiwa, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Negative Electrodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research interest in Na-ion batteries has increased rapidly because of the environmental friendliness of sodium compared to lithium.  A review of recent scientific advances in the field of neg. electrode materials used for Na-ion batteries.  This paper sheds light on neg. electrode materials for Na-ion batteries: carbonaceous materials, oxides/phosphates (as sodium insertion materials), sodium alloy/compds. and so on.  These electrode materials have different reaction mechanisms for electrochem. sodiation/desodiation processes.  Moreover, not only sodiation-active materials but also binders, current collectors, electrolytes and electrode/electrolyte interphase and its stabilization are essential for long cycle life Na-ion batteries. Benefiting from the two-dimensional (2D) nanoarchitecture of Ga2O3 NSs and rGO, Ga2O3 NSs/rGO nanocomposites exhibit enhanced kinetics and improved cycling stability in Li-ion and Na-ion batteries (LIBs/SIBs).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cu2S is considered as a promising electrode material for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries owing to its flat charge-discharge plateau as well as the abundant reserves.  However, serious capacity fading and formation of polysulfides during electrochem. process restrict its practical application.  In this work, a new type of Cu2S@N, S dual-doped carbon matrix (Cu2S@NSCm) hybrid is synthesized through a simple in-situ polymn. process and subsequent carbonization process.  Due to the N, S dual-doped carbon matrix, which can buffer the vol. change, restrain the dissoln. of polysulfide and enhance the electron cond. during electrochem. process, the hybrid demonstrates excellent electrochem. performance.  The Cu2S@NSCm hybrid exhibits a reversible capacity of 560.1 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 1000 mA g-1 after 550 cycles when used in lithium-ion batteries, which is among the best performance for Cu2S based anode materials.  Moreover, a capacity of 182.3 mAh g-1 is obtained after 50 cycles when used as an anode material in sodium ion batteries, which is much better than the pure Cu2S.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqK-qlB0cmT7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljn-fK0TdPE9Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtVSgsb%252FI&amp;md5=135a8e335420e7d73978fad847de8c8f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b05618"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Phosphorus-Doped Hard Carbon Nanofibers Prepared by Electrospinning as an Anode in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus-doped hard carbon nanofibers with macroporous structure were successfully synthesized by electrospinning followed by a thermal treatment process using polyacrylonitrile and H3PO4 as carbon and phosphorus precursors, resp.  XPS anal. reveals that the doped phosphorus atoms can incorporate into the carbon framework and most of them are connecting with carbon atoms to form P-C bond.  The (002) plane interlayer spacing was taken from the X-ray diffraction pattern, which shows a large spacing of 3.83 Å for the obtained P-doped hard carbon nanofibers.  When used as an anode in sodium-ion batteries, the as-prepd. P-doped hard carbon nanofibers can deliver a reversible capacity of 288 and 103 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 and 2 A g-1, resp.  After 200 cycles at 50 mA g-1, the capacity retention of P-doped hard carbon nanofibers still reaches 87.8%, demonstrating good cycling durability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Boosting electrochem. sodium storage properties is achieved by utilizing functionalized N-doped carbon nanotube arrays (NCNAs) as anode materials.  The NCNA anodes are first fabricated by self-polymn. of dopamine on cobalt hydroxide nanorod arrays as the template.  The NCNAs with diams. of 100-120 nm are grown vertically to Ni foam, forming self-supported nanotube arrays.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.  Understanding of the reaction mechanism between Sn and Na ions will provide insight towards exploring and designing new alloy-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqL8pwZXJa53LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUYd7hSKLSdQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsFKhtr8%253D&amp;md5=7fba4efb710816f1ca877f0494169a46</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1021/nn502045y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lotfabad, E.                         rightTab-cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Density Sodium and Lithium Ion Battery Anodes from Banana Peels</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Ding, Jia; Cui, Kai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Kalisvaart, W. Peter; Hazelton, Michael; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7115-7129</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. ∼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material ∼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. ∼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat ∼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with ∼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material ∼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode ∼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvndZF6mFnK7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUYd7hSKLSdQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpt1Krt70%253D&amp;md5=1ad531caaafd48d9545b9c814029eada</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsuda, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishii, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cumings, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Expanded Graphite as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         rightTab-cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUYd7hSKLSdQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXksVeqs7g%253D&amp;md5=e4bc2edac8d22121e7986e165b7df007</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201604108"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">S-Doped N-Rich Carbon Nanosheets with Expanded Interlayer Distance as Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">N-doped porous carbon sheets were successfully fabricated by chem. activation (KOH) of polypyrrole-functionalized graphene sheets.  Unique compositional and structural features endow the N-doped porous carbon sheets with superior Na ion battery (NIB) performance, i.e., a high reversible capacity (349.7 mAhg-1 at a c.d. of 50 mAg-1), a good cycling stability (260 cycles), and a high rate capability (50 mAhg-1 even at a very high c.d. of 20 Ag-1). However, some inherent serious issues (e.g., low elec. cond., narrow pore size, relatively low pore vol., etc.) are still obsd. for nitrogen-doped porous carbon particles, which restrict their CDI performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Uniquely structured Fe2O3 and FeSe2/graphitic-C (GC) microrods composed of hollow Fe2O3 and FeSe2 nanospheres, resp., were successfully prepd. by applying a metal-org. framework (MOF, MIL-88) as the precursor and template. In the case of H-FeSe2/GC, the C framework not only prevents the structural collapse but also ensures sufficient electron transport during repeated cycles. Hierarchically porous N-doped carbon polyhedrons anchored on crumpled graphene balls (NPC/CGBs) are synthesized by carbonizing a zeolitic imidazolate framework-8 (ZIF-8)/CGB composite precursor, producing an unprecedented effective host matrix for high-performance Li-Se batteries. However, the lack of efficient and inexpensive catalysts or catalyst systems that can compete with noble metal catalysts hinders their large-scale industrial applications.  As an important class of porous materials, metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) with systematically tailored structures and compns. have recently been suggested as promising precursors for the prepn. of diverse functional materials.  Here we report a dual-MOF confined-pyrolysis approach for the prepn. of iron carbide nanoparticle-embedded carbon nanotube assemblies.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising energy storage device, and anode materials are proved to essentially affect their electrochem. performance.  In this work, a yolk-shell structured Co9S8/MoS2 polyhedron with N-doped carbon composite (Co9S8/MoS2-CN) was designed and synthesized through a step by step process using ZIF-67 as the precursor.  And this composite is demonstrated to be an excellent electrode candidate material for SIBs due to the magnificent yolk-shelled structures, in situ CN coating and the synergistic effect of the binary sulfides.  The Co9S8/MoS2-CN composite electrode shows highly stable cycling performance and delivers a reversible discharge capacity of 438 mA h g-1 within 150 cycles at a c.d. of 1.0 A g-1, which is much higher than that of Co9S8-CN or MoS2 electrodes. Shahriar A.; Pan, Zheng-Ze; Xiang, Bin; Yang, Quan-Hong; Yamauchi, Yusuke</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">29</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15356-15366</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) have gained significant attention as precursors for the fabrication of porous hybrid materials due to their highly controllable compn., structure and pore size.  However, at present, MOF-derived materials have rarely been studied as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The authors report the fabrication of a Ni-doped Co/CoO/N-doped C (NC) hybrid using bimetallic Ni-Co-ZIF as the starting precursor.  The resulting Ni-doped Co/CoO/NC hybrid is highly microporous with a high sp. surface area of 552 m2 g-1.  When employed as an anode material for Na-ion batteries, the Ni-doped Co/CoO/NC hybrid exhibited both good rate performance with a high discharge capacity of 218 mA h g-1 at a high c.d. of 500 mA g-1 and good cycling stability, as a high discharge capacity of 218.7 mA h g-1 can be retained after 100 cycles at 500 mA g-1, corresponding to a high capacity retention of 87.5%.  The excellent electrochem. performance of the Ni-doped Co/CoO/NC hybrid for SIBs may be attributed to the synergistic effects of various factors, including: (a) the presence of a C matrix which provides protection against aggregation and pulverization during sodiation/desodiation; (b) the highly microporous nature along with the presence of a few mesopores which facilitates better insertion/de-insertion of Na+ ions; (c) the Ni-doping which introduces defect sites into the at. structure of CoO via partial substitution, thus enhancing the cond. of the Co oxide (CoO) component and hence, the overall hybrid material, and (d) the N-doping which promotes a faster migration speed of Na ions (Na+) across the C layer by creating defect sites, thereby improving the cond. of the C frameworks in the hybrid material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqWR9Qrpy6E07Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhouAni012nng"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na metal batteries have been regarded as promising candidates for energy storage systems due to their high energy d. and the widespread availability of low cost sodium.  However, the practical application of metallic sodium anode is impeded by its low stripping/plating efficiency and poor cycling performance due to the poor reversibility of sodium anode caused by the formation of inhomogeneous solid electrolyte interphase, the growth of dendritic sodium, and relatively infinite vol. change.  Here, Na-C composite anode was fabricated by depositing nanoscale metallic sodium in graphitized carbon microspheres which were assembled from graphitized carbon nanosheets.  The carbon microspheres function as a mini-nanoreservoir with high-surface-area, cond., and mech. stability, which lower the local c.d., ensure a homogeneous Na nucleation and high electrochem. active of Na, and restrict the vol. change.  As a result, metallic sodium can be reversibly nondendritic stripped/plated with a high Coulombic efficiency of 99.3% up to 4mA cm-2 for 4mA h cm-2. Conductive N-doped C with slit pores uniformly coated on the TiO2 spheres surface.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium (Na) metal is an ideal anode for high-energy Na batteries due to the low cost and natural abundance of Na metal.  Nevertheless, issues regarding dendritic and mossy Na metal deposits have prevented their practical application.  Herein, nitrogen and oxygen co-doped graphitized carbon fibers (DGCF) have been developed as the Na plating hosts to direct Na metal homogeneous nucleation and suppress the growth of Na dendrites.  Exptl. results as well as first-principles calcns. demonstrate that the uniformly doped nitrogen and oxygen function as sodiophilic sites that direct the sodium-metal nucleation to a smooth dendrite-free anode.  The resultant DGCF-Na anode can be cycled stably at 1 mA cm-2 for a high areal capacity of 12.7 mA h cm-2 with an av. Coulombic efficiency of 99.8%, and a Na|Na sym. cell can be cycled with long-term durability for &gt; 1200 h at 2 mA cm-2.  When coupled with P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn1/3Ti1/3O2 and Na3V2(PO4)3 cathodes, the DGCF-Na composite demonstrates good feasibility in full cells.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoXV-C0XgL4UbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhouAni012nng"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsFertbvL&amp;md5=9b2c31c3884f09413321d52cfd28c521</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201500195"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qie, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sulfur-Doped Carbon with Enlarged Interlayer Distance as a High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Acute toxicity of mercury in food is a challenging problem in the health and environmental sectors which could be addressed by designing robust org. porous materials contg. strong mercury chelating sites with high affinity and rapid uptake capacity towards mercury contaminants.  Taking the intrinsic advantages of hypercrosslinked polymers, we have developed porous materials having chelating thiophene units in the pore walls.  As a proof-of-concept, the bottom-up synthesis approach is used for Friedel-Crafts polymn. of thiophene in making Th-1, Th-2 and Th-3 as representative materials.  Among these porous materials, Th-2 exhibits high efficiency in removing mercury from aq. soln., affording a Hg(II) uptake capacity of 145 mg g-1.  The high adsorption capacity renders rapid sepn. of Hg(II) from aq. soln., resulting in a concn. well below the acceptable value in drinking water.  The impressive performance might be because of the synergistic action between densely packed thiophene sites and rapid diffusion throughout the well-defined micropores and interparticle mesopores.  The interaction mechanism between Hg(II) and Th-2 has been established through XPS and FT-IR studies.  Overall, we have explored the potential of hypercrosslinked thiophene polymers as scavengers of mercury for the first time and this may be extended for the treatment of mercury contaminated waste water in the future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqwajvEInnShLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljiX3mAlHtIkg"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">As an efficient method to promote the electrochem. performances of carbon materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), heteroatom (S, N, etc.) doping has attracted significant interest from researchers, and great efforts have been devoted to explore the functional mechanism of SIBs.  Heteroatom doping enlarges the layer distance of carbon, provides more active sites, and enhances the cond. for the promotion of sodium storage; however, the influences of heteroatom-contg. precursors on the structures, morphologies and electrochem. sodium storage performances of carbon materials have rarely been explored.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nitrogen-doped bamboo-like carbon nanotubes (N-BCNTs) were synthesized using a facile one-step pyrolysis process.  Due to their unique one-dimensional hollow structure and intrinsic high nitrogen content, N-BCNTs exhibit high capacity, superior rate capability, and excellent cycle stability and are, thus, promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqKD91JkU3YI7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljiX3mAlHtIkg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVGisrjM&amp;md5=d70fea3e9a84ab5c2869ae11bcda5ee2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE01985K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A High Performance Sulfur-Doped Disordered Carbon Anode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2916</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2921</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C5EE01985K</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A high performance sulfur-doped disordered carbon anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Wei; Zhou, Min; Li, Haomiao; Wang, Kangli; Cheng, Shijie; Jiang, Kai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2916-2921</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sulfur-doped disordered carbon is facilely synthesized and investigated as an anode for sodium ion batteries.  Benefiting from the high sulfur doping (∼26.9 wt%), it demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 516 mA h g-1, excellent rate capability as well as superior cycling stability (271 mA h g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 1000 cycles).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrSwmZlK_1JZ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljiX3mAlHtIkg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlejsLrL&amp;md5=8225ee76874fd51a3bcd976a8550ad65</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b06290"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of Mesoporous Co<sup>2+</sup>-Doped TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanodisks Derived from Metal Organic Frameworks with Improved Sodium Storage Performance</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>9</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">32071</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">32079</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.7b06290</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of Mesoporous Co2+-Doped TiO2 Nanodisks Derived from Metal Organic Frameworks with Improved Sodium Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Zhensheng; Kang, Meiling; Chen, Xiaohui; Zhou, Kaiqiang; Huang, Zhigao; Wei, Mingdeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">32071-32079</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">TiO2 is a most promising anode candidate for rechargeable Na-ion batteries (NIBs) due to its appropriate working voltage, low cost and superior structural stability during charge/discharge process.  Nevertheless, it suffers from intrinsically low elec. cond.  Herein, we report an in-situ synthesis of Co2+ doped TiO2 through the thermal treatment of metal org. frameworks precursors of MIL-125(Ti)-Co as a superior anode material for NIBs.  The Co2+ doped TiO2 possesses uniform nanodisk morphol., a large surface area and mesoporous structure with narrow pore distribution.  The reversible capacity, Coulombic efficiency (CE) and rate capability can be improved by Co2+ doping in mesoporous TiO2 anode.  Co2+ doped mesoporous TiO2 nanodisks exhibited a high reversible capacity of 232 mAhg-1 at 0.1 Ag-1, good rate capability and cycling stability with a stable capacity of about 140 mAhg-1 at 0.5 Ag-1 after 500 cycles.  The enhanced Na-ion storage performance could be due to the increased elec. cond. revealed by Kelvin probe force microscopy measurements.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqeI-xy5CaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljiX3mAlHtIkg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtFCmtLrL&amp;md5=fd756e424ef0cadadbde38cff356ae8e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA00236F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Z. G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Superior Sodium-Ion Storage Performance of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@Nitrogen-Doped Carbon: Derived from a Metal-Organic Framework</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5428</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5435</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6TA00236F</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Superior sodium-ion storage performance of Co3O4@nitrogen-doped carbon: derived from a metal-organic framework</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Ying; Wang, Caiyun; Wang, Yijing; Liu, Huakun; Huang, Zhenguo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5428-5435</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">N-doped C coated Co3O4 nanoparticles (Co3O4@NC) with high Na-ion storage capacity and unprecedented long-life cycling stability are reported.  The Co3O4@NC was derived from a metal-org. framework ZIF-67, where the Co ions and org. linkers were, resp., converted to Co3O4 nanoparticle cores and N-doped C shells through a controlled 2-step annealing process.  The Co3O4@NC shows a porous nature with a surface area of 101 m2 g-1.  When applied as an anode for Na ion batteries (SIBs), Co3O4@NC delivers a high reversible capacity of 506, 317, and 263 mA h g-1 at 100, 400, and 1000 mA g-1, resp.  A capacity degrdn. of 0.03% per cycle over 1100 cycles was achieved at a high c.d. of 1000 mA g-1.  The outstanding Na-ion storage performance can be ascribed to the N-doped C coating (NC), which facilitates the capacitive reaction, minimizes the vol. changes of Co3O4, and also enhances the electronic cond.  This work sheds light on how to develop high-performance metal oxide@NC nanocomposites for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr9srwVE0kC6bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljl7GLk-HsEtQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslWjtLg%253D&amp;md5=36f8823fd3051b22c5e45e40e63400bc</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div analysis, HAADF-STEM and elemental mappings of the MDC-700, CV and discharge–charge curves of MDC-700 and SC-700, cycling stability of 3DSC-700, MDC-700, and SC-700 at 100 mA/g, cycling stability of 8/SC-700,67/SC-700, and 3DSC-700 at 100 and 5000 mA/g, rate performance of 8/SC-700, 67/SC-700, and 3DSC-700 at various current densities (1000–5000 mA/g), all relevant calculation equations for pseudocapacitive contributions, elemental analysis of 3DSC-700 and MDC-700, and comparison of the electrochemical performance (<a (function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {

	});

	});

<script nonce="90f6ec7b0c6e775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

)|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>
